GET on that same target resource will result in an equivalent
representation being sent in a [200 (OK)](#status.200){.xref} response.
However, there is no guarantee that such a state change will be
observable, since the target resource might be acted upon by other user
agents in parallel, or might be subject to dynamic processing by the
origin server, before any subsequent GET is received. A successful
response only implies that the user agent\'s intent was achieved at the
time of its processing by the origin
server.[¶](#section-9.3.4-1){.pilcrow}

If the target resource does not have a current representation and the
PUT successfully creates one, then the origin server [MUST]{.bcp14}
inform the user agent by sending a [201 (Created)](#status.201){.xref}
response. If the target resource does have a current representation and
that representation is successfully modified in accordance with the
state of the enclosed representation, then the origin server
[MUST]{.bcp14} send either a [200 (OK)](#status.200){.xref} or a [204
(No Content)](#status.204){.xref} response to indicate successful
completion of the request.[¶](#section-9.3.4-2){.pilcrow}

An origin server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} verify that the PUT representation is
consistent with its configured constraints for the target resource. For
example, if an origin server determines a resource\'s representation
metadata based on the URI, then the origin server needs to ensure that
the content received in a successful PUT request is consistent with that
metadata. When a PUT representation is inconsistent with the target
resource, the origin server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} either make them
consistent, by transforming the representation or changing the resource
configuration, or respond with an appropriate error message containing
sufficient information to explain why the representation is unsuitable.
The [409 (Conflict)](#status.409){.xref} or [415 (Unsupported Media
Type)](#status.415){.xref} status codes are suggested, with the latter
being specific to constraints on
[Content-Type](#field.content-type){.xref}
values.[¶](#section-9.3.4-3){.pilcrow}

For example, if the target resource is configured to always have a
[Content-Type](#field.content-type){.xref} of \"text/html\" and the
representation being PUT has a Content-Type of \"image/jpeg\", the
origin server ought to do one of:[¶](#section-9.3.4-4){.pilcrow}

a.  [reconfigure the target resource to reflect the new media
    type;[¶](#section-9.3.4-5.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-9.3.4-5.1}
b.  [transform the PUT representation to a format consistent with that
    of the resource before saving it as the new resource state;
    or,[¶](#section-9.3.4-5.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-9.3.4-5.2}
c.  [reject the request with a [415 (Unsupported Media
    Type)](#status.415){.xref} response indicating that the target
    resource is limited to \"text/html\", perhaps including a link to a
    different resource that would be a suitable target for the new
    representation.[¶](#section-9.3.4-5.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-9.3.4-5.3}

HTTP does not define exactly how a PUT method affects the state of an
origin server beyond what can be expressed by the intent of the user
agent request and the semantics of the origin server response. It does
not define what a resource might be, in any sense of that word, beyond
the interface provided via HTTP. It does not define how resource state
is \"stored\", nor how such storage might change as a result of a change
in resource state, nor how the origin server translates resource state
into representations. Generally speaking, all implementation details
behind the resource interface are intentionally hidden by the
server.[¶](#section-9.3.4-6){.pilcrow}

This extends to how header and trailer fields are stored; while common
header fields like [Content-Type](#field.content-type){.xref} will
typically be stored and returned upon subsequent GET requests, header
and trailer field handling is specific to the resource that received the
request. As a result, an origin server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} ignore
unrecognized header and trailer fields received in a PUT request (i.e.,
not save them as part of the resource
state).[¶](#section-9.3.4-7){.pilcrow}

An origin server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send a validator field ([Section
8.8](#response.validator){.xref}), such as an [ETag](#field.etag){.xref}
or [Last-Modified](#field.last-modified){.xref} field, in a successful
response to PUT unless the request\'s representation data was saved
without any transformation applied to the content (i.e., the resource\'s
new representation data is identical to the content received in the PUT
request) and the validator field value reflects the new representation.
This requirement allows a user agent to know when the representation it
sent (and retains in memory) is the result of the PUT, and thus it
doesn\'t need to be retrieved again from the origin server. The new
validator(s) received in the response can be used for future conditional
requests in order to prevent accidental overwrites ([Section
13.1](#preconditions){.xref}).[¶](#section-9.3.4-8){.pilcrow}

The fundamental difference between the POST and PUT methods is
highlighted by the different intent for the enclosed representation. The
target resource in a POST request is intended to handle the enclosed
representation according to the resource\'s own semantics, whereas the
enclosed representation in a PUT request is defined as replacing the
state of the target resource. Hence, the intent of PUT is idempotent and
visible to intermediaries, even though the exact effect is only known by
the origin server.[¶](#section-9.3.4-9){.pilcrow}

Proper interpretation of a PUT request presumes that the user agent
knows which target resource is desired. A service that selects a proper
URI on behalf of the client, after receiving a state-changing request,
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} be implemented using the POST method rather than PUT.
If the origin server will not make the requested PUT state change to the
target resource and instead wishes to have it applied to a different
resource, such as when the resource has been moved to a different URI,
then the origin server [MUST]{.bcp14} send an appropriate [3xx
(Redirection)](#status.3xx){.xref} response; the user agent
[MAY]{.bcp14} then make its own decision regarding whether or not to
redirect the request.[¶](#section-9.3.4-10){.pilcrow}

A PUT request applied to the target resource can have side effects on
other resources. For example, an article might have a URI for
identifying \"the current version\" (a resource) that is separate from
the URIs identifying each particular version (different resources that
at one point shared the same state as the current version resource). A
successful PUT request on \"the current version\" URI might therefore
create a new version resource in addition to changing the state of the
target resource, and might also cause links to be added between the
related resources.[¶](#section-9.3.4-11){.pilcrow}

Some origin servers support use of the
[Content-Range](#field.content-range){.xref} header field ([Section
14.4](#field.content-range){.xref}) as a request modifier to perform a
partial PUT, as described in [Section
14.5](#partial.PUT){.xref}.[¶](#section-9.3.4-12){.pilcrow}

Responses to the PUT method are not cacheable. If a successful PUT
request passes through a cache that has one or more stored responses for
the target URI, those stored responses will be invalidated (see [Section
4.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9111#section-4.4){.relref} of
\[[CACHING](#CACHING){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-9.3.4-13){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#DELETE}
::: {#section-9.3.5 .section}
#### [9.3.5.](#section-9.3.5){.section-number .selfRef} [DELETE](#name-delete){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-delete}

[]{#iref-delete-method-226 .iref} []{#iref-method-delete-227 .iref}

The DELETE method requests that the origin server remove the association
between the [target resource](#target.resource){.xref} and its current
functionality. In effect, this method is similar to the \"rm\" command
in UNIX: it expresses a deletion operation on the URI mapping of the
origin server rather than an expectation that the previously associated
information be deleted.[¶](#section-9.3.5-1){.pilcrow}

If the target resource has one or more current representations, they
might or might not be destroyed by the origin server, and the associated
storage might or might not be reclaimed, depending entirely on the
nature of the resource and its implementation by the origin server
(which are beyond the scope of this specification). Likewise, other
implementation aspects of a resource might need to be deactivated or
archived as a result of a DELETE, such as database or gateway
connections. In general, it is assumed that the origin server will only
allow DELETE on resources for which it has a prescribed mechanism for
accomplishing the deletion.[¶](#section-9.3.5-2){.pilcrow}

Relatively few resources allow the DELETE method \-- its primary use is
for remote authoring environments, where the user has some direction
regarding its effect. For example, a resource that was previously
created using a PUT request, or identified via the Location header field
after a [201 (Created)](#status.201){.xref} response to a POST request,
might allow a corresponding DELETE request to undo those actions.
Similarly, custom user agent implementations that implement an authoring
function, such as revision control clients using HTTP for remote
operations, might use DELETE based on an assumption that the server\'s
URI space has been crafted to correspond to a version
repository.[¶](#section-9.3.5-3){.pilcrow}

If a DELETE method is successfully applied, the origin server
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} send[¶](#section-9.3.5-4){.pilcrow}

-   [a [202 (Accepted)](#status.202){.xref} status code if the action
    will likely succeed but has not yet been
    enacted,[¶](#section-9.3.5-5.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-9.3.5-5.1}
-   [a [204 (No Content)](#status.204){.xref} status code if the action
    has been enacted and no further information is to be supplied,
    or[¶](#section-9.3.5-5.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-9.3.5-5.2}
-   [a [200 (OK)](#status.200){.xref} status code if the action has been
    enacted and the response message includes a representation
    describing the
    status.[¶](#section-9.3.5-5.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-9.3.5-5.3}

Although request message framing is independent of the method used,
content received in a DELETE request has no generally defined semantics,
cannot alter the meaning or target of the request, and might lead some
implementations to reject the request and close the connection because
of its potential as a request smuggling attack ([Section
11.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9112#section-11.2){.relref} of
\[[HTTP/1.1](#HTTP11){.xref}\]). A client [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} generate
content in a DELETE request unless it is made directly to an origin
server that has previously indicated, in or out of band, that such a
request has a purpose and will be adequately supported. An origin server
[SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} rely on private agreements to receive content,
since participants in HTTP communication are often unaware of
intermediaries along the request chain.[¶](#section-9.3.5-6){.pilcrow}

Responses to the DELETE method are not cacheable. If a successful DELETE
request passes through a cache that has one or more stored responses for
the target URI, those stored responses will be invalidated (see [Section
4.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9111#section-4.4){.relref} of
\[[CACHING](#CACHING){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-9.3.5-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#CONNECT}
::: {#section-9.3.6 .section}
#### [9.3.6.](#section-9.3.6){.section-number .selfRef} [CONNECT](#name-connect){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-connect}

[]{#iref-connect-method-228 .iref} []{#iref-method-connect-229 .iref}

The CONNECT method requests that the recipient establish a tunnel to the
destination origin server identified by the request target and, if
successful, thereafter restrict its behavior to blind forwarding of
data, in both directions, until the tunnel is closed. Tunnels are
commonly used to create an end-to-end virtual connection, through one or
more proxies, which can then be secured using TLS (Transport Layer
Security, \[[TLS13](#TLS13){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-9.3.6-1){.pilcrow}

CONNECT uses a special form of request target, unique to this method,
consisting of only the host and port number of the tunnel destination,
separated by a colon. There is no default port; a client [MUST]{.bcp14}
send the port number even if the CONNECT request is based on a URI
reference that contains an authority component with an elided port
([Section 4.1](#uri.references){.xref}). For
example,[¶](#section-9.3.6-2){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-9.3.6-3}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
CONNECT server.example.com:80 HTTP/1.1
Host: server.example.com
```

[¶](#section-9.3.6-3){.pilcrow}
:::

A server [MUST]{.bcp14} reject a CONNECT request that targets an empty
or invalid port number, typically by responding with a 400 (Bad Request)
status code.[¶](#section-9.3.6-4){.pilcrow}

Because CONNECT changes the request/response nature of an HTTP
connection, specific HTTP versions might have different ways of mapping
its semantics into the protocol\'s wire
format.[¶](#section-9.3.6-5){.pilcrow}

CONNECT is intended for use in requests to a proxy. The recipient can
establish a tunnel either by directly connecting to the server
identified by the request target or, if configured to use another proxy,
by forwarding the CONNECT request to the next inbound proxy. An origin
server [MAY]{.bcp14} accept a CONNECT request, but most origin servers
do not implement CONNECT.[¶](#section-9.3.6-6){.pilcrow}

Any [2xx (Successful)](#status.2xx){.xref} response indicates that the
sender (and all inbound proxies) will switch to tunnel mode immediately
after the response header section; data received after that header
section is from the server identified by the request target. Any
response other than a successful response indicates that the tunnel has
not yet been formed.[¶](#section-9.3.6-7){.pilcrow}

A tunnel is closed when a tunnel intermediary detects that either side
has closed its connection: the intermediary [MUST]{.bcp14} attempt to
send any outstanding data that came from the closed side to the other
side, close both connections, and then discard any remaining data left
undelivered.[¶](#section-9.3.6-8){.pilcrow}

Proxy authentication might be used to establish the authority to create
a tunnel. For example,[¶](#section-9.3.6-9){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-9.3.6-10}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
CONNECT server.example.com:443 HTTP/1.1
Host: server.example.com:443
Proxy-Authorization: basic aGVsbG86d29ybGQ=
```

[¶](#section-9.3.6-10){.pilcrow}
:::

There are significant risks in establishing a tunnel to arbitrary
servers, particularly when the destination is a well-known or reserved
TCP port that is not intended for Web traffic. For example, a CONNECT to
\"example.com:25\" would suggest that the proxy connect to the reserved
port for SMTP traffic; if allowed, that could trick the proxy into
relaying spam email. Proxies that support CONNECT [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
restrict its use to a limited set of known ports or a configurable list
of safe request targets.[¶](#section-9.3.6-11){.pilcrow}

A server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send any Transfer-Encoding or
[Content-Length](#field.content-length){.xref} header fields in a [2xx
(Successful)](#status.2xx){.xref} response to CONNECT. A client
[MUST]{.bcp14} ignore any Content-Length or Transfer-Encoding header
fields received in a successful response to
CONNECT.[¶](#section-9.3.6-12){.pilcrow}

A CONNECT request message does not have content. The interpretation of
data sent after the header section of the CONNECT request message is
specific to the version of HTTP in use.[¶](#section-9.3.6-13){.pilcrow}

Responses to the CONNECT method are not
cacheable.[¶](#section-9.3.6-14){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#OPTIONS}
::: {#section-9.3.7 .section}
#### [9.3.7.](#section-9.3.7){.section-number .selfRef} [OPTIONS](#name-options){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-options}

[]{#iref-options-method-230 .iref} []{#iref-method-options-231 .iref}

The OPTIONS method requests information about the communication options
available for the target resource, at either the origin server or an
intervening intermediary. This method allows a client to determine the
options and/or requirements associated with a resource, or the
capabilities of a server, without implying a resource
action.[¶](#section-9.3.7-1){.pilcrow}

An OPTIONS request with an asterisk (\"\*\") as the request target
([Section 7.1](#target.resource){.xref}) applies to the server in
general rather than to a specific resource. Since a server\'s
communication options typically depend on the resource, the \"\*\"
request is only useful as a \"ping\" or \"no-op\" type of method; it
does nothing beyond allowing the client to test the capabilities of the
server. For example, this can be used to test a proxy for HTTP/1.1
conformance (or lack thereof).[¶](#section-9.3.7-2){.pilcrow}

If the request target is not an asterisk, the OPTIONS request applies to
the options that are available when communicating with the target
resource.[¶](#section-9.3.7-3){.pilcrow}

A server generating a successful response to OPTIONS [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
send any header that might indicate optional features implemented by the
server and applicable to the target resource (e.g.,
[Allow](#field.allow){.xref}), including potential extensions not
defined by this specification. The response content, if any, might also
describe the communication options in a machine or human-readable
representation. A standard format for such a representation is not
defined by this specification, but might be defined by future extensions
to HTTP.[¶](#section-9.3.7-4){.pilcrow}

A client [MAY]{.bcp14} send a [Max-Forwards](#field.max-forwards){.xref}
header field in an OPTIONS request to target a specific recipient in the
request chain (see [Section 7.6.2](#field.max-forwards){.xref}). A proxy
[MUST NOT]{.bcp14} generate a Max-Forwards header field while forwarding
a request unless that request was received with a Max-Forwards
field.[¶](#section-9.3.7-5){.pilcrow}

A client that generates an OPTIONS request containing content
[MUST]{.bcp14} send a valid [Content-Type](#field.content-type){.xref}
header field describing the representation media type. Note that this
specification does not define any use for such
content.[¶](#section-9.3.7-6){.pilcrow}

Responses to the OPTIONS method are not
cacheable.[¶](#section-9.3.7-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#TRACE}
::: {#section-9.3.8 .section}
#### [9.3.8.](#section-9.3.8){.section-number .selfRef} [TRACE](#name-trace){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-trace}

[]{#iref-trace-method-232 .iref} []{#iref-method-trace-233 .iref}

The TRACE method requests a remote, application-level loop-back of the
request message. The final recipient of the request [SHOULD]{.bcp14}
reflect the message received, excluding some fields described below,
back to the client as the content of a [200 (OK)](#status.200){.xref}
response. The \"message/http\" format ([Section
10.1](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9112#section-10.1){.relref} of
\[[HTTP/1.1](#HTTP11){.xref}\]) is one way to do so. The final recipient
is either the origin server or the first server to receive a
[Max-Forwards](#field.max-forwards){.xref} value of zero (0) in the
request ([Section
7.6.2](#field.max-forwards){.xref}).[¶](#section-9.3.8-1){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} generate fields in a TRACE request
containing sensitive data that might be disclosed by the response. For
example, it would be foolish for a user agent to send stored user
credentials ([Section 11](#authentication){.xref}) or cookies
\[[COOKIE](#COOKIE){.xref}\] in a TRACE request. The final recipient of
the request [SHOULD]{.bcp14} exclude any request fields that are likely
to contain sensitive data when that recipient generates the response
content.[¶](#section-9.3.8-2){.pilcrow}

TRACE allows the client to see what is being received at the other end
of the request chain and use that data for testing or diagnostic
information. The value of the [Via](#field.via){.xref} header field
([Section 7.6.3](#field.via){.xref}) is of particular interest, since it
acts as a trace of the request chain. Use of the
[Max-Forwards](#field.max-forwards){.xref} header field allows the
client to limit the length of the request chain, which is useful for
testing a chain of proxies forwarding messages in an infinite
loop.[¶](#section-9.3.8-3){.pilcrow}

A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send content in a TRACE
request.[¶](#section-9.3.8-4){.pilcrow}

Responses to the TRACE method are not
cacheable.[¶](#section-9.3.8-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#context}
::: {#section-10 .section}
## [10.](#section-10){.section-number .selfRef} [Message Context](#name-message-context){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-message-context}

::: {#request.context}
::: {#section-10.1 .section}
### [10.1.](#section-10.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Request Context Fields](#name-request-context-fields){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-request-context-fields}

The request header fields below provide additional information about the
request context, including information about the user, user agent, and
resource behind the request.[¶](#section-10.1-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#field.expect}
::: {#section-10.1.1 .section}
#### [10.1.1.](#section-10.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Expect](#name-expect){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-expect}

[]{#iref-fields-expect-234 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-expect-235
.iref} []{#iref-expect-header-field-236 .iref}
[]{#iref-100-continue-expect-value-2 .iref}

The \"Expect\" header field in a request indicates a certain set of
behaviors (expectations) that need to be supported by the server in
order to properly handle this request.[¶](#section-10.1.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-expect-238 .iref}

::: {#section-10.1.1-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Expect =      #expectation
  expectation = token [ "=" ( token / quoted-string ) parameters ]
```

[¶](#section-10.1.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::

The Expect field value is
case-insensitive.[¶](#section-10.1.1-3){.pilcrow}

The only expectation defined by this specification is \"100-continue\"
(with no defined parameters).[¶](#section-10.1.1-4){.pilcrow}

A server that receives an Expect field value containing a member other
than [100-continue](#field.expect){.xref} [MAY]{.bcp14} respond with a
[417 (Expectation Failed)](#status.417){.xref} status code to indicate
that the unexpected expectation cannot be
met.[¶](#section-10.1.1-5){.pilcrow}

A \"100-continue\" expectation informs recipients that the client is
about to send (presumably large) content in this request and wishes to
receive a [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref} interim response if the
method, target URI, and header fields are not sufficient to cause an
immediate success, redirect, or error response. This allows the client
to wait for an indication that it is worthwhile to send the content
before actually doing so, which can improve efficiency when the data is
huge or when the client anticipates that an error is likely (e.g., when
sending a state-changing method, for the first time, without previously
verified authentication credentials).[¶](#section-10.1.1-6){.pilcrow}

For example, a request that begins with[¶](#section-10.1.1-7){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.1.1-8}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
PUT /somewhere/fun HTTP/1.1
Host: origin.example.com
Content-Type: video/h264
Content-Length: 1234567890987
Expect: 100-continue
```

[¶](#section-10.1.1-8){.pilcrow}
:::

allows the origin server to immediately respond with an error message,
such as [401 (Unauthorized)](#status.401){.xref} or [405 (Method Not
Allowed)](#status.405){.xref}, before the client starts filling the
pipes with an unnecessary data transfer.[¶](#section-10.1.1-9){.pilcrow}

Requirements for clients:[¶](#section-10.1.1-10){.pilcrow}

-   [A client [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} generate a 100-continue expectation in
    a request that does not include
    content.[¶](#section-10.1.1-11.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-11.1}
-   [A client that will wait for a [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref}
    response before sending the request content [MUST]{.bcp14} send an
    [Expect](#field.expect){.xref} header field containing a
    100-continue
    expectation.[¶](#section-10.1.1-11.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-11.2}
-   [A client that sends a 100-continue expectation is not required to
    wait for any specific length of time; such a client [MAY]{.bcp14}
    proceed to send the content even if it has not yet received a
    response. Furthermore, since [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref}
    responses cannot be sent through an HTTP/1.0 intermediary, such a
    client [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} wait for an indefinite period before
    sending the
    content.[¶](#section-10.1.1-11.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-11.3}
-   [A client that receives a [417 (Expectation
    Failed)](#status.417){.xref} status code in response to a request
    containing a 100-continue expectation [SHOULD]{.bcp14} repeat that
    request without a 100-continue expectation, since the 417 response
    merely indicates that the response chain does not support
    expectations (e.g., it passes through an HTTP/1.0
    server).[¶](#section-10.1.1-11.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-11.4}

Requirements for servers:[¶](#section-10.1.1-12){.pilcrow}

-   [A server that receives a 100-continue expectation in an HTTP/1.0
    request [MUST]{.bcp14} ignore that
    expectation.[¶](#section-10.1.1-13.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-13.1}
-   [A server [MAY]{.bcp14} omit sending a [100
    (Continue)](#status.100){.xref} response if it has already received
    some or all of the content for the corresponding request, or if the
    framing indicates that there is no
    content.[¶](#section-10.1.1-13.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-13.2}
-   [A server that sends a [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref} response
    [MUST]{.bcp14} ultimately send a final status code, once it receives
    and processes the request content, unless the connection is closed
    prematurely.[¶](#section-10.1.1-13.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-13.3}
-   [A server that responds with a final status code before reading the
    entire request content [SHOULD]{.bcp14} indicate whether it intends
    to close the connection (e.g., see [Section
    9.6](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9112#section-9.6){.relref} of
    \[[HTTP/1.1](#HTTP11){.xref}\]) or continue reading the request
    content.[¶](#section-10.1.1-13.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-13.4}

Upon receiving an HTTP/1.1 (or later) request that has a method, target
URI, and complete header section that contains a 100-continue
expectation and an indication that request content will follow, an
origin server [MUST]{.bcp14} send
either:[¶](#section-10.1.1-14){.pilcrow}

-   [an immediate response with a final status code, if that status can
    be determined by examining just the method, target URI, and header
    fields, or[¶](#section-10.1.1-15.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-15.1}
-   [an immediate [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref} response to
    encourage the client to send the request
    content.[¶](#section-10.1.1-15.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-15.2}

The origin server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} wait for the content before sending
the [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref}
response.[¶](#section-10.1.1-16){.pilcrow}

Upon receiving an HTTP/1.1 (or later) request that has a method, target
URI, and complete header section that contains a 100-continue
expectation and indicates a request content will follow, a proxy
[MUST]{.bcp14} either:[¶](#section-10.1.1-17){.pilcrow}

-   [send an immediate response with a final status code, if that status
    can be determined by examining just the method, target URI, and
    header fields,
    or[¶](#section-10.1.1-18.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-18.1}
-   [forward the request toward the origin server by sending a
    corresponding request-line and header section to the next inbound
    server.[¶](#section-10.1.1-18.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-10.1.1-18.2}

If the proxy believes (from configuration or past interaction) that the
next inbound server only supports HTTP/1.0, the proxy [MAY]{.bcp14}
generate an immediate [100 (Continue)](#status.100){.xref} response to
encourage the client to begin sending the
content.[¶](#section-10.1.1-19){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.from}
::: {#section-10.1.2 .section}
#### [10.1.2.](#section-10.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [From](#name-from){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-from}

[]{#iref-fields-from-239 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-from-240 .iref}
[]{#iref-from-header-field-241 .iref}

The \"From\" header field contains an Internet email address for a human
user who controls the requesting user agent. The address ought to be
machine-usable, as defined by \"mailbox\" in [Section
3.4](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5322#section-3.4){.relref} of
\[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\]:[¶](#section-10.1.2-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-from-242 .iref}

::: {#section-10.1.2-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  From    = mailbox

  mailbox = <mailbox, see [RFC5322], Section 3.4>
```

[¶](#section-10.1.2-2){.pilcrow}
:::

An example is:[¶](#section-10.1.2-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.1.2-4}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
From: spider-admin@example.org
```

[¶](#section-10.1.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::

The From header field is rarely sent by non-robotic user agents. A user
agent [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} send a From header field without explicit
configuration by the user, since that might conflict with the user\'s
privacy interests or their site\'s security
policy.[¶](#section-10.1.2-5){.pilcrow}

A robotic user agent [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send a valid From header field so
that the person responsible for running the robot can be contacted if
problems occur on servers, such as if the robot is sending excessive,
unwanted, or invalid requests.[¶](#section-10.1.2-6){.pilcrow}

A server [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} use the From header field for access
control or authentication, since its value is expected to be visible to
anyone receiving or observing the request and is often recorded within
logfiles and error reports without any expectation of
privacy.[¶](#section-10.1.2-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.referer}
::: {#section-10.1.3 .section}
#### [10.1.3.](#section-10.1.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Referer](#name-referer){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-referer}

[]{#iref-fields-referer-243 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-referer-244
.iref} []{#iref-referer-header-field-245 .iref}

The \"Referer\" \[sic\] header field allows the user agent to specify a
URI reference for the resource from which the [target
URI](#target.resource){.xref} was obtained (i.e., the \"referrer\",
though the field name is misspelled). A user agent [MUST NOT]{.bcp14}
include the fragment and userinfo components of the URI reference
\[[URI](#URI){.xref}\], if any, when generating the Referer field
value.[¶](#section-10.1.3-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-referer-246 .iref}

::: {#section-10.1.3-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Referer = absolute-URI / partial-URI
```

[¶](#section-10.1.3-2){.pilcrow}
:::

The field value is either an [absolute-URI](#uri.references){.xref} or a
[partial-URI](#uri.references){.xref}. In the latter case ([Section
4](#uri){.xref}), the referenced URI is relative to the target URI
(\[[URI](#URI){.xref}\], [Section
5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3986#section-5){.relref}).[¶](#section-10.1.3-3){.pilcrow}

The Referer header field allows servers to generate back-links to other
resources for simple analytics, logging, optimized caching, etc. It also
allows obsolete or mistyped links to be found for maintenance. Some
servers use the Referer header field as a means of denying links from
other sites (so-called \"deep linking\") or restricting cross-site
request forgery (CSRF), but not all requests contain
it.[¶](#section-10.1.3-4){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-10.1.3-5){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.1.3-6}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
Referer: http://www.example.org/hypertext/Overview.html
```

[¶](#section-10.1.3-6){.pilcrow}
:::

If the target URI was obtained from a source that does not have its own
URI (e.g., input from the user keyboard, or an entry within the user\'s
bookmarks/favorites), the user agent [MUST]{.bcp14} either exclude the
Referer header field or send it with a value of
\"about:blank\".[¶](#section-10.1.3-7){.pilcrow}

The Referer header field value need not convey the full URI of the
referring resource; a user agent [MAY]{.bcp14} truncate parts other than
the referring origin.[¶](#section-10.1.3-8){.pilcrow}

The Referer header field has the potential to reveal information about
the request context or browsing history of the user, which is a privacy
concern if the referring resource\'s identifier reveals personal
information (such as an account name) or a resource that is supposed to
be confidential (such as behind a firewall or internal to a secured
service). Most general-purpose user agents do not send the Referer
header field when the referring resource is a local \"file\" or \"data\"
URI. A user agent [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} send a
[Referer](#field.referer){.xref} header field if the referring resource
was accessed with a secure protocol and the request target has an origin
differing from that of the referring resource, unless the referring
resource explicitly allows Referer to be sent. A user agent [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} send a [Referer](#field.referer){.xref} header field in an
unsecured HTTP request if the referring resource was accessed with a
secure protocol. See [Section
17.9](#sensitive.information.in.uris){.xref} for additional security
considerations.[¶](#section-10.1.3-9){.pilcrow}

Some intermediaries have been known to indiscriminately remove Referer
header fields from outgoing requests. This has the unfortunate side
effect of interfering with protection against CSRF attacks, which can be
far more harmful to their users. Intermediaries and user agent
extensions that wish to limit information disclosure in Referer ought to
restrict their changes to specific edits, such as replacing internal
domain names with pseudonyms or truncating the query and/or path
components. An intermediary [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} modify or delete the
Referer header field when the field value shares the same scheme and
host as the target URI.[¶](#section-10.1.3-10){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.te}
::: {#section-10.1.4 .section}
#### [10.1.4.](#section-10.1.4){.section-number .selfRef} [TE](#name-te){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-te}

[]{#iref-fields-te-247 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-te-248 .iref}
[]{#iref-te-header-field-249 .iref}

The \"TE\" header field describes capabilities of the client with regard
to transfer codings and trailer
sections.[¶](#section-10.1.4-1){.pilcrow}

As described in [Section 6.5](#trailer.fields){.xref}, a TE field with a
\"trailers\" member sent in a request indicates that the client will not
discard trailer fields.[¶](#section-10.1.4-2){.pilcrow}

TE is also used within HTTP/1.1 to advise servers about which transfer
codings the client is able to accept in a response. As of publication,
only HTTP/1.1 uses transfer codings (see [Section
7](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9112#section-7){.relref} of
\[[HTTP/1.1](#HTTP11){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-10.1.4-3){.pilcrow}

The TE field value is a list of members, with each member (aside from
\"trailers\") consisting of a transfer coding name token with an
optional weight indicating the client\'s relative preference for that
transfer coding ([Section 12.4.2](#quality.values){.xref}) and optional
parameters for that transfer coding.[¶](#section-10.1.4-4){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-te-250 .iref} []{#iref-grammar-t-codings-251 .iref}
[]{#iref-grammar-transfer-coding-252 .iref}
[]{#iref-grammar-transfer-parameter- .iref}

::: {#section-10.1.4-5}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  TE                 = #t-codings
  t-codings          = "trailers" / ( transfer-coding [ weight ] )
  transfer-coding    = token *( OWS ";" OWS transfer-parameter )
  transfer-parameter = token BWS "=" BWS ( token / quoted-string )
```

[¶](#section-10.1.4-5){.pilcrow}
:::

A sender of TE [MUST]{.bcp14} also send a \"TE\" connection option
within the [Connection](#field.connection){.xref} header field ([Section
7.6.1](#field.connection){.xref}) to inform intermediaries not to
forward this field.[¶](#section-10.1.4-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.user-agent}
::: {#section-10.1.5 .section}
#### [10.1.5.](#section-10.1.5){.section-number .selfRef} [User-Agent](#name-user-agent){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-user-agent}

[]{#iref-fields-user-agent-254 .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-user-agent-25 .iref}
[]{#iref-user-agent-header-field-256 .iref}

The \"User-Agent\" header field contains information about the user
agent originating the request, which is often used by servers to help
identify the scope of reported interoperability problems, to work around
or tailor responses to avoid particular user agent limitations, and for
analytics regarding browser or operating system use. A user agent
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} send a User-Agent header field in each request unless
specifically configured not to do so.[¶](#section-10.1.5-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-user-agent-257 .iref}

::: {#section-10.1.5-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  User-Agent = product *( RWS ( product / comment ) )
```

[¶](#section-10.1.5-2){.pilcrow}
:::

The User-Agent field value consists of one or more product identifiers,
each followed by zero or more comments ([Section
5.6.5](#comments){.xref}), which together identify the user agent
software and its significant subproducts. By convention, the product
identifiers are listed in decreasing order of their significance for
identifying the user agent software. Each product identifier consists of
a name and optional version.[¶](#section-10.1.5-3){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-product-258 .iref} []{#iref-grammar-product-version-259
.iref}

::: {#section-10.1.5-4}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  product         = token ["/" product-version]
  product-version = token
```

[¶](#section-10.1.5-4){.pilcrow}
:::

A sender [SHOULD]{.bcp14} limit generated product identifiers to what is
necessary to identify the product; a sender [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} generate
advertising or other nonessential information within the product
identifier. A sender [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} generate information in
[product-version](#field.user-agent){.xref} that is not a version
identifier (i.e., successive versions of the same product name ought to
differ only in the product-version portion of the product
identifier).[¶](#section-10.1.5-5){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-10.1.5-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.1.5-7}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
User-Agent: CERN-LineMode/2.15 libwww/2.17b3
```

[¶](#section-10.1.5-7){.pilcrow}
:::

A user agent [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} generate a User-Agent header field
containing needlessly fine-grained detail and [SHOULD]{.bcp14} limit the
addition of subproducts by third parties. Overly long and detailed
User-Agent field values increase request latency and the risk of a user
being identified against their wishes
(\"fingerprinting\").[¶](#section-10.1.5-8){.pilcrow}

Likewise, implementations are encouraged not to use the product tokens
of other implementations in order to declare compatibility with them, as
this circumvents the purpose of the field. If a user agent masquerades
as a different user agent, recipients can assume that the user
intentionally desires to see responses tailored for that identified user
agent, even if they might not work as well for the actual user agent
being used.[¶](#section-10.1.5-9){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#response.context}
::: {#section-10.2 .section}
### [10.2.](#section-10.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Response Context Fields](#name-response-context-fields){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-response-context-fields}

The response header fields below provide additional information about
the response, beyond what is implied by the status code, including
information about the server, about the [target
resource](#target.resource){.xref}, or about related
resources.[¶](#section-10.2-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#field.allow}
::: {#section-10.2.1 .section}
#### [10.2.1.](#section-10.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Allow](#name-allow){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-allow}

[]{#iref-fields-allow-260 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-allow-261 .iref}
[]{#iref-allow-header-field-262 .iref}

The \"Allow\" header field lists the set of methods advertised as
supported by the [target resource](#target.resource){.xref}. The purpose
of this field is strictly to inform the recipient of valid request
methods associated with the resource.[¶](#section-10.2.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-allow-263 .iref}

::: {#section-10.2.1-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Allow = #method
```

[¶](#section-10.2.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::

Example of use:[¶](#section-10.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.2.1-4}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
Allow: GET, HEAD, PUT
```

[¶](#section-10.2.1-4){.pilcrow}
:::

The actual set of allowed methods is defined by the origin server at the
time of each request. An origin server [MUST]{.bcp14} generate an Allow
header field in a [405 (Method Not Allowed)](#status.405){.xref}
response and [MAY]{.bcp14} do so in any other response. An empty Allow
field value indicates that the resource allows no methods, which might
occur in a 405 response if the resource has been temporarily disabled by
configuration.[¶](#section-10.2.1-5){.pilcrow}

A proxy [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} modify the Allow header field \-- it does not
need to understand all of the indicated methods in order to handle them
according to the generic message handling
rules.[¶](#section-10.2.1-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.location}
::: {#section-10.2.2 .section}
#### [10.2.2.](#section-10.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Location](#name-location){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-location}

[]{#iref-fields-location-264 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-location-265
.iref} []{#iref-location-header-field-266 .iref}

The \"Location\" header field is used in some responses to refer to a
specific resource in relation to the response. The type of relationship
is defined by the combination of request method and status code
semantics.[¶](#section-10.2.2-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-location-267 .iref}

::: {#section-10.2.2-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Location = URI-reference
```

[¶](#section-10.2.2-2){.pilcrow}
:::

The field value consists of a single URI-reference. When it has the form
of a relative reference (\[[URI](#URI){.xref}\], [Section
4.2](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3986#section-4.2){.relref}), the
final value is computed by resolving it against the target URI
(\[[URI](#URI){.xref}\], [Section
5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3986#section-5){.relref}).[¶](#section-10.2.2-3){.pilcrow}

For [201 (Created)](#status.201){.xref} responses, the Location value
refers to the primary resource created by the request. For [3xx
(Redirection)](#status.3xx){.xref} responses, the Location value refers
to the preferred target resource for automatically redirecting the
request.[¶](#section-10.2.2-4){.pilcrow}

If the Location value provided in a [3xx
(Redirection)](#status.3xx){.xref} response does not have a fragment
component, a user agent [MUST]{.bcp14} process the redirection as if the
value inherits the fragment component of the URI reference used to
generate the target URI (i.e., the redirection inherits the original
reference\'s fragment, if any).[¶](#section-10.2.2-5){.pilcrow}

For example, a GET request generated for the URI reference
\"http://www.example.org/\~tim\" might result in a [303 (See
Other)](#status.303){.xref} response containing the header
field:[¶](#section-10.2.2-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.2.2-7}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
Location: /People.html#tim
```

[¶](#section-10.2.2-7){.pilcrow}
:::

which suggests that the user agent redirect to
\"http://www.example.org/People.html#tim\"[¶](#section-10.2.2-8){.pilcrow}

Likewise, a GET request generated for the URI reference
\"http://www.example.org/index.html#larry\" might result in a [301
(Moved Permanently)](#status.301){.xref} response containing the header
field:[¶](#section-10.2.2-9){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.2.2-10}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
Location: http://www.example.net/index.html
```

[¶](#section-10.2.2-10){.pilcrow}
:::

which suggests that the user agent redirect to
\"http://www.example.net/index.html#larry\", preserving the original
fragment identifier.[¶](#section-10.2.2-11){.pilcrow}

There are circumstances in which a fragment identifier in a Location
value would not be appropriate. For example, the Location header field
in a [201 (Created)](#status.201){.xref} response is supposed to provide
a URI that is specific to the created
resource.[¶](#section-10.2.2-12){.pilcrow}

**Note:** Some recipients attempt to recover from Location header fields
that are not valid URI references. This specification does not mandate
or define such processing, but does allow it for the sake of robustness.
A Location field value cannot allow a list of members because the comma
list separator is a valid data character within a URI-reference. If an
invalid message is sent with multiple Location field lines, a recipient
along the path might combine those field lines into one value. Recovery
of a valid Location field value from that situation is difficult and not
interoperable across implementations.[¶](#section-10.2.2-13.1){.pilcrow}

**Note:** The [Content-Location](#field.content-location){.xref} header
field ([Section 8.7](#field.content-location){.xref}) differs from
Location in that the Content-Location refers to the most specific
resource corresponding to the enclosed representation. It is therefore
possible for a response to contain both the Location and
Content-Location header fields.[¶](#section-10.2.2-14.1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.retry-after}
::: {#section-10.2.3 .section}
#### [10.2.3.](#section-10.2.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Retry-After](#name-retry-after){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-retry-after}

[]{#iref-fields-retry-after-268 .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-retry-after-2 .iref}
[]{#iref-retry-after-header-field-27 .iref}

Servers send the \"Retry-After\" header field to indicate how long the
user agent ought to wait before making a follow-up request. When sent
with a [503 (Service Unavailable)](#status.503){.xref} response,
Retry-After indicates how long the service is expected to be unavailable
to the client. When sent with any [3xx
(Redirection)](#status.3xx){.xref} response, Retry-After indicates the
minimum time that the user agent is asked to wait before issuing the
redirected request.[¶](#section-10.2.3-1){.pilcrow}

The Retry-After field value can be either an HTTP-date or a number of
seconds to delay after receiving the
response.[¶](#section-10.2.3-2){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-retry-after-271 .iref}

::: {#section-10.2.3-3}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Retry-After = HTTP-date / delay-seconds
```

[¶](#section-10.2.3-3){.pilcrow}
:::

::: {#rule.delay-seconds}
A delay-seconds value is a non-negative decimal integer, representing
time in seconds.[¶](#section-10.2.3-4){.pilcrow}
:::

[]{#iref-grammar-delay-seconds-272 .iref}

::: {#section-10.2.3-5}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  delay-seconds  = 1*DIGIT
```

[¶](#section-10.2.3-5){.pilcrow}
:::

Two examples of its use are[¶](#section-10.2.3-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.2.3-7}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
Retry-After: Fri, 31 Dec 1999 23:59:59 GMT
Retry-After: 120
```

[¶](#section-10.2.3-7){.pilcrow}
:::

In the latter example, the delay is 2
minutes.[¶](#section-10.2.3-8){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.server}
::: {#section-10.2.4 .section}
#### [10.2.4.](#section-10.2.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Server](#name-server){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server}

[]{#iref-fields-server-273 .iref} []{#iref-header-fields-server-274
.iref} []{#iref-server-header-field-275 .iref}

The \"Server\" header field contains information about the software used
by the origin server to handle the request, which is often used by
clients to help identify the scope of reported interoperability
problems, to work around or tailor requests to avoid particular server
limitations, and for analytics regarding server or operating system use.
An origin server [MAY]{.bcp14} generate a Server header field in its
responses.[¶](#section-10.2.4-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-server-276 .iref}

::: {#section-10.2.4-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Server = product *( RWS ( product / comment ) )
```

[¶](#section-10.2.4-2){.pilcrow}
:::

The Server header field value consists of one or more product
identifiers, each followed by zero or more comments ([Section
5.6.5](#comments){.xref}), which together identify the origin server
software and its significant subproducts. By convention, the product
identifiers are listed in decreasing order of their significance for
identifying the origin server software. Each product identifier consists
of a name and optional version, as defined in [Section
10.1.5](#field.user-agent){.xref}.[¶](#section-10.2.4-3){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-10.2.4-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-10.2.4-5}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
Server: CERN/3.0 libwww/2.17
```

[¶](#section-10.2.4-5){.pilcrow}
:::

An origin server [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} generate a Server header field
containing needlessly fine-grained detail and [SHOULD]{.bcp14} limit the
addition of subproducts by third parties. Overly long and detailed
Server field values increase response latency and potentially reveal
internal implementation details that might make it (slightly) easier for
attackers to find and exploit known security
holes.[¶](#section-10.2.4-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#authentication}
::: {#section-11 .section}
## [11.](#section-11){.section-number .selfRef} [HTTP Authentication](#name-http-authentication){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-http-authentication}

::: {#auth.scheme}
::: {#section-11.1 .section}
### [11.1.](#section-11.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Authentication Scheme](#name-authentication-scheme){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authentication-scheme}

HTTP provides a general framework for access control and authentication,
via an extensible set of challenge-response authentication schemes,
which can be used by a server to challenge a client request and by a
client to provide authentication information. It uses a case-insensitive
token to identify the authentication
scheme:[¶](#section-11.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-auth-scheme-277 .iref}

::: {#section-11.1-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  auth-scheme    = token
```

[¶](#section-11.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::

Aside from the general framework, this document does not specify any
authentication schemes. New and existing authentication schemes are
specified independently and ought to be registered within the
\"Hypertext Transfer Protocol (HTTP) Authentication Scheme Registry\".
For example, the \"basic\" and \"digest\" authentication schemes are
defined by \[[RFC7617](#RFC7617){.xref}\] and
\[[RFC7616](#RFC7616){.xref}\],
respectively.[¶](#section-11.1-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#auth.params}
::: {#section-11.2 .section}
### [11.2.](#section-11.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Authentication Parameters](#name-authentication-parameters){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authentication-parameters}

The authentication scheme is followed by additional information
necessary for achieving authentication via that scheme as either a
comma-separated list of parameters or a single sequence of characters
capable of holding base64-encoded
information.[¶](#section-11.2-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-token68-278 .iref}

::: {#section-11.2-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  token68        = 1*( ALPHA / DIGIT /
                       "-" / "." / "_" / "~" / "+" / "/" ) *"="
```

[¶](#section-11.2-2){.pilcrow}
:::

The token68 syntax allows the 66 unreserved URI characters
(\[[URI](#URI){.xref}\]), plus a few others, so that it can hold a
base64, base64url (URL and filename safe alphabet), base32, or base16
(hex) encoding, with or without padding, but excluding whitespace
(\[[RFC4648](#RFC4648){.xref}\]).[¶](#section-11.2-3){.pilcrow}

Authentication parameters are name/value pairs, where the name token is
matched case-insensitively and each parameter name [MUST]{.bcp14} only
occur once per challenge.[¶](#section-11.2-4){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-auth-param-279 .iref}

::: {#section-11.2-5}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  auth-param     = token BWS "=" BWS ( token / quoted-string )
```

[¶](#section-11.2-5){.pilcrow}
:::

Parameter values can be expressed either as \"token\" or as
\"quoted-string\" ([Section 5.6](#fields.components){.xref}).
Authentication scheme definitions need to accept both notations, both
for senders and recipients, to allow recipients to use generic parsing
components regardless of the authentication
scheme.[¶](#section-11.2-6){.pilcrow}

For backwards compatibility, authentication scheme definitions can
restrict the format for senders to one of the two variants. This can be
important when it is known that deployed implementations will fail when
encountering one of the two formats.[¶](#section-11.2-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#challenge.and.response}
::: {#section-11.3 .section}
### [11.3.](#section-11.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Challenge and Response](#name-challenge-and-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-challenge-and-response}

A [401 (Unauthorized)](#status.401){.xref} response message is used by
an origin server to challenge the authorization of a user agent,
including a [WWW-Authenticate](#field.www-authenticate){.xref} header
field containing at least one challenge applicable to the requested
resource.[¶](#section-11.3-1){.pilcrow}

A [407 (Proxy Authentication Required)](#status.407){.xref} response
message is used by a proxy to challenge the authorization of a client,
including a [Proxy-Authenticate](#field.proxy-authenticate){.xref}
header field containing at least one challenge applicable to the proxy
for the requested resource.[¶](#section-11.3-2){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-challenge-280 .iref}

::: {#section-11.3-3}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  challenge   = auth-scheme [ 1*SP ( token68 / #auth-param ) ]
```

[¶](#section-11.3-3){.pilcrow}
:::

**Note:** Many clients fail to parse a challenge that contains an
unknown scheme. A workaround for this problem is to list well-supported
schemes (such as \"basic\") first.[¶](#section-11.3-4.1){.pilcrow}

A user agent that wishes to authenticate itself with an origin server
\-- usually, but not necessarily, after receiving a [401
(Unauthorized)](#status.401){.xref} \-- can do so by including an
[Authorization](#field.authorization){.xref} header field with the
request.[¶](#section-11.3-5){.pilcrow}

A client that wishes to authenticate itself with a proxy \-- usually,
but not necessarily, after receiving a [407 (Proxy Authentication
Required)](#status.407){.xref} \-- can do so by including a
[Proxy-Authorization](#field.proxy-authorization){.xref} header field
with the request.[¶](#section-11.3-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#credentials}
::: {#section-11.4 .section}
### [11.4.](#section-11.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Credentials](#name-credentials){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-credentials}

Both the [Authorization](#field.authorization){.xref} field value and
the [Proxy-Authorization](#field.proxy-authorization){.xref} field value
contain the client\'s credentials for the realm of the resource being
requested, based upon a challenge received in a response (possibly at
some point in the past). When creating their values, the user agent
ought to do so by selecting the challenge with what it considers to be
the most secure auth-scheme that it understands, obtaining credentials
from the user as appropriate. Transmission of credentials within header
field values implies significant security considerations regarding the
confidentiality of the underlying connection, as described in [Section
17.16.1](#confidentiality.of.credentials){.xref}.[¶](#section-11.4-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-credentials-281 .iref}

::: {#section-11.4-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  credentials = auth-scheme [ 1*SP ( token68 / #auth-param ) ]
```

[¶](#section-11.4-2){.pilcrow}
:::

Upon receipt of a request for a protected resource that omits
credentials, contains invalid credentials (e.g., a bad password) or
partial credentials (e.g., when the authentication scheme requires more
than one round trip), an origin server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send a [401
(Unauthorized)](#status.401){.xref} response that contains a
[WWW-Authenticate](#field.www-authenticate){.xref} header field with at
least one (possibly new) challenge applicable to the requested
resource.[¶](#section-11.4-3){.pilcrow}

Likewise, upon receipt of a request that omits proxy credentials or
contains invalid or partial proxy credentials, a proxy that requires
authentication [SHOULD]{.bcp14} generate a [407 (Proxy Authentication
Required)](#status.407){.xref} response that contains a
[Proxy-Authenticate](#field.proxy-authenticate){.xref} header field with
at least one (possibly new) challenge applicable to the
proxy.[¶](#section-11.4-4){.pilcrow}

A server that receives valid credentials that are not adequate to gain
access ought to respond with the [403 (Forbidden)](#status.403){.xref}
status code ([Section
15.5.4](#status.403){.xref}).[¶](#section-11.4-5){.pilcrow}

HTTP does not restrict applications to this simple challenge-response
framework for access authentication. Additional mechanisms can be used,
such as authentication at the transport level or via message
encapsulation, and with additional header fields specifying
authentication information. However, such additional mechanisms are not
defined by this specification.[¶](#section-11.4-6){.pilcrow}

Note that various custom mechanisms for user authentication use the
Set-Cookie and Cookie header fields, defined in
\[[COOKIE](#COOKIE){.xref}\], for passing tokens related to
authentication.[¶](#section-11.4-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#protection.space}
::: {#section-11.5 .section}
### [11.5.](#section-11.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Establishing a Protection Space (Realm)](#name-establishing-a-protection-s){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-establishing-a-protection-s}

[]{#iref-protection-space-282 .iref} []{#iref-realm-283 .iref}
[]{#iref-origin-284 .iref}

The \"realm\" authentication parameter is reserved for use by
authentication schemes that wish to indicate a scope of
protection.[¶](#section-11.5-1){.pilcrow}

A \"protection space\" is defined by the origin (see [Section
4.3.1](#origin){.xref}) of the server being accessed, in combination
with the realm value if present. These realms allow the protected
resources on a server to be partitioned into a set of protection spaces,
each with its own authentication scheme and/or authorization database.
The realm value is a string, generally assigned by the origin server,
that can have additional semantics specific to the authentication
scheme. Note that a response can have multiple challenges with the same
auth-scheme but with different realms.[¶](#section-11.5-2){.pilcrow}

The protection space determines the domain over which credentials can be
automatically applied. If a prior request has been authorized, the user
agent [MAY]{.bcp14} reuse the same credentials for all other requests
within that protection space for a period of time determined by the
authentication scheme, parameters, and/or user preferences (such as a
configurable inactivity timeout).[¶](#section-11.5-3){.pilcrow}

The extent of a protection space, and therefore the requests to which
credentials might be automatically applied, is not necessarily known to
clients without additional information. An authentication scheme might
define parameters that describe the extent of a protection space. Unless
specifically allowed by the authentication scheme, a single protection
space cannot extend outside the scope of its
server.[¶](#section-11.5-4){.pilcrow}

For historical reasons, a sender [MUST]{.bcp14} only generate the
quoted-string syntax. Recipients might have to support both token and
quoted-string syntax for maximum interoperability with existing clients
that have been accepting both notations for a long
time.[¶](#section-11.5-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#auth.user.origin.server}
::: {#section-11.6 .section}
### [11.6.](#section-11.6){.section-number .selfRef} [Authenticating Users to Origin Servers](#name-authenticating-users-to-ori){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authenticating-users-to-ori}

::: {#field.www-authenticate}
::: {#section-11.6.1 .section}
#### [11.6.1.](#section-11.6.1){.section-number .selfRef} [WWW-Authenticate](#name-www-authenticate){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-www-authenticate}

[]{#iref-fields-www-authenticate-285 .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-www-authentic .iref}
[]{#iref-www-authenticate-header-fie .iref}

The \"WWW-Authenticate\" response header field indicates the
authentication scheme(s) and parameters applicable to the target
resource.[¶](#section-11.6.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-www-authenticate-28 .iref}

::: {#section-11.6.1-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  WWW-Authenticate = #challenge
```

[¶](#section-11.6.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::

A server generating a [401 (Unauthorized)](#status.401){.xref} response
[MUST]{.bcp14} send a WWW-Authenticate header field containing at least
one challenge. A server [MAY]{.bcp14} generate a WWW-Authenticate header
field in other response messages to indicate that supplying credentials
(or different credentials) might affect the
response.[¶](#section-11.6.1-3){.pilcrow}

A proxy forwarding a response [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} modify any
[WWW-Authenticate](#field.www-authenticate){.xref} header fields in that
response.[¶](#section-11.6.1-4){.pilcrow}

User agents are advised to take special care in parsing the field value,
as it might contain more than one challenge, and each challenge can
contain a comma-separated list of authentication parameters.
Furthermore, the header field itself can occur multiple
times.[¶](#section-11.6.1-5){.pilcrow}

For instance:[¶](#section-11.6.1-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-11.6.1-7}
``` {.lang-http-message .sourcecode}
WWW-Authenticate: Basic realm="simple", Newauth realm="apps",
                 type=1, title="Login to \"apps\""
```

[¶](#section-11.6.1-7){.pilcrow}
:::

This header field contains two challenges, one for the \"Basic\" scheme
with a realm value of \"simple\" and another for the \"Newauth\" scheme
with a realm value of \"apps\". It also contains two additional
parameters, \"type\" and \"title\".[¶](#section-11.6.1-8){.pilcrow}

Some user agents do not recognize this form, however. As a result,
sending a WWW-Authenticate field value with more than one member on the
same field line might not be
interoperable.[¶](#section-11.6.1-9){.pilcrow}

**Note:** The challenge grammar production uses the list syntax as well.
Therefore, a sequence of comma, whitespace, and comma can be considered
either as applying to the preceding challenge, or to be an empty entry
in the list of challenges. In practice, this ambiguity does not affect
the semantics of the header field value and thus is
harmless.[¶](#section-11.6.1-10.1){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.authorization}
::: {#section-11.6.2 .section}
#### [11.6.2.](#section-11.6.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Authorization](#name-authorization){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authorization}

[]{#iref-fields-authorization-289 .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-authorization .iref}
[]{#iref-authorization-header-field- .iref}

The \"Authorization\" header field allows a user agent to authenticate
itself with an origin server \-- usually, but not necessarily, after
receiving a [401 (Unauthorized)](#status.401){.xref} response. Its value
consists of credentials containing the authentication information of the
user agent for the realm of the resource being
requested.[¶](#section-11.6.2-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-authorization-292 .iref}

::: {#section-11.6.2-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Authorization = credentials
```

[¶](#section-11.6.2-2){.pilcrow}
:::

If a request is authenticated and a realm specified, the same
credentials are presumed to be valid for all other requests within this
realm (assuming that the authentication scheme itself does not require
otherwise, such as credentials that vary according to a challenge value
or using synchronized clocks).[¶](#section-11.6.2-3){.pilcrow}

A proxy forwarding a request [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} modify any
[Authorization](#field.authorization){.xref} header fields in that
request. See [Section
3.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9111#section-3.5){.relref} of
\[[CACHING](#CACHING){.xref}\] for details of and requirements
pertaining to handling of the Authorization header field by HTTP
caches.[¶](#section-11.6.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.authentication-info}
::: {#section-11.6.3 .section}
#### [11.6.3.](#section-11.6.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Authentication-Info](#name-authentication-info){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authentication-info}

[]{#iref-fields-authentication-info- .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-authenticatio .iref}
[]{#iref-authentication-info-header- .iref}

HTTP authentication schemes can use the \"Authentication-Info\" response
field to communicate information after the client\'s authentication
credentials have been accepted. This information can include a
finalization message from the server (e.g., it can contain the server
authentication).[¶](#section-11.6.3-1){.pilcrow}

The field value is a list of parameters (name/value pairs), using the
\"auth-param\" syntax defined in [Section
11.3](#challenge.and.response){.xref}. This specification only describes
the generic format; authentication schemes using Authentication-Info
will define the individual parameters. The \"Digest\" Authentication
Scheme, for instance, defines multiple parameters in [Section
3.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7616#section-3.5){.relref} of
\[[RFC7616](#RFC7616){.xref}\].[¶](#section-11.6.3-2){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-authentication-info .iref}

::: {#section-11.6.3-3}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Authentication-Info = #auth-param
```

[¶](#section-11.6.3-3){.pilcrow}
:::

The Authentication-Info field can be used in any HTTP response,
independently of request method and status code. Its semantics are
defined by the authentication scheme indicated by the
[Authorization](#field.authorization){.xref} header field ([Section
11.6.2](#field.authorization){.xref}) of the corresponding
request.[¶](#section-11.6.3-4){.pilcrow}

A proxy forwarding a response is not allowed to modify the field value
in any way.[¶](#section-11.6.3-5){.pilcrow}

Authentication-Info can be sent as a trailer field ([Section
6.5](#trailer.fields){.xref}) when the authentication scheme explicitly
allows this.[¶](#section-11.6.3-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#auth.client.proxy}
::: {#section-11.7 .section}
### [11.7.](#section-11.7){.section-number .selfRef} [Authenticating Clients to Proxies](#name-authenticating-clients-to-p){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-authenticating-clients-to-p}

::: {#field.proxy-authenticate}
::: {#section-11.7.1 .section}
#### [11.7.1.](#section-11.7.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Proxy-Authenticate](#name-proxy-authenticate){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-proxy-authenticate}

[]{#iref-fields-proxy-authenticate-2 .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-proxy-authent .iref}
[]{#iref-proxy-authenticate-header-f .iref}

The \"Proxy-Authenticate\" header field consists of at least one
challenge that indicates the authentication scheme(s) and parameters
applicable to the proxy for this request. A proxy [MUST]{.bcp14} send at
least one Proxy-Authenticate header field in each [407 (Proxy
Authentication Required)](#status.407){.xref} response that it
generates.[¶](#section-11.7.1-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-proxy-authenticate- .iref}

::: {#section-11.7.1-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Proxy-Authenticate = #challenge
```

[¶](#section-11.7.1-2){.pilcrow}
:::

Unlike [WWW-Authenticate](#field.www-authenticate){.xref}, the
Proxy-Authenticate header field applies only to the next outbound client
on the response chain. This is because only the client that chose a
given proxy is likely to have the credentials necessary for
authentication. However, when multiple proxies are used within the same
administrative domain, such as office and regional caching proxies
within a large corporate network, it is common for credentials to be
generated by the user agent and passed through the hierarchy until
consumed. Hence, in such a configuration, it will appear as if
Proxy-Authenticate is being forwarded because each proxy will send the
same challenge set.[¶](#section-11.7.1-3){.pilcrow}

Note that the parsing considerations for
[WWW-Authenticate](#field.www-authenticate){.xref} apply to this header
field as well; see [Section 11.6.1](#field.www-authenticate){.xref} for
details.[¶](#section-11.7.1-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.proxy-authorization}
::: {#section-11.7.2 .section}
#### [11.7.2.](#section-11.7.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Proxy-Authorization](#name-proxy-authorization){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-proxy-authorization}

[]{#iref-fields-proxy-authorization- .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-proxy-authori .iref}
[]{#iref-proxy-authorization-header- .iref}

The \"Proxy-Authorization\" header field allows the client to identify
itself (or its user) to a proxy that requires authentication. Its value
consists of credentials containing the authentication information of the
client for the proxy and/or realm of the resource being
requested.[¶](#section-11.7.2-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-proxy-authorization .iref}

::: {#section-11.7.2-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Proxy-Authorization = credentials
```

[¶](#section-11.7.2-2){.pilcrow}
:::

Unlike [Authorization](#field.authorization){.xref}, the
Proxy-Authorization header field applies only to the next inbound proxy
that demanded authentication using the
[Proxy-Authenticate](#field.proxy-authenticate){.xref} header field.
When multiple proxies are used in a chain, the Proxy-Authorization
header field is consumed by the first inbound proxy that was expecting
to receive credentials. A proxy [MAY]{.bcp14} relay the credentials from
the client request to the next proxy if that is the mechanism by which
the proxies cooperatively authenticate a given
request.[¶](#section-11.7.2-3){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#field.proxy-authentication-info}
::: {#section-11.7.3 .section}
#### [11.7.3.](#section-11.7.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Proxy-Authentication-Info](#name-proxy-authentication-info){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-proxy-authentication-info}

[]{#iref-fields-proxy-authentication .iref}
[]{#iref-header-fields-proxy-authenti .iref}
[]{#iref-proxy-authentication-info-h .iref}

The \"Proxy-Authentication-Info\" response header field is equivalent to
[Authentication-Info](#field.authentication-info){.xref}, except that it
applies to proxy authentication ([Section
11.3](#challenge.and.response){.xref}) and its semantics are defined by
the authentication scheme indicated by the Proxy-Authorization header
field ([Section 11.7.2](#field.proxy-authorization){.xref}) of the
corresponding request:[¶](#section-11.7.3-1){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-grammar-proxy-authenticatio .iref}

::: {#section-11.7.3-2}
``` {.lang-abnf9110 .sourcecode}
  Proxy-Authentication-Info = #auth-param
```

[¶](#section-11.7.3-2){.pilcrow}
:::

However, unlike
[Authentication-Info](#field.authentication-info){.xref}, the
Proxy-Authentication-Info header field applies only to the next outbound
client on the response chain. This is because only the client that chose
a given proxy is likely to have the credentials necessary for
authentication. However, when multiple proxies are used within the same
administrative domain, such as office and regional caching proxies
within a large corporate network, it is common for credentials to be
generated by the user agent and passed through the hierarchy until
consumed. Hence, in such a configuration, it will appear as if
Proxy-Authentication-Info is being forwarded because each proxy will
send the same field value.[¶](#section-11.7.3-3){.pilcrow}

Proxy-Authentication-Info can be sent as a trailer field ([Section
6.5](#trailer.fields){.xref}) when the authentication scheme explicitly
allows this.[¶](#section-11.7.3-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#content.negotiation}
::: {#section-12 .section}
## [12.](#section-12){.section-number .selfRef} [Content Negotiation](#name-content-negotiation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-content-negotiation}

When responses convey content, whether indicating a success or an error,
the origin server often has different ways of representing that
information; for example, in different formats, languages, or encodings.
Likewise, different users or user agents might have differing
capabilities, characteristics, or preferences that could influence which
representation, among those available, would be best to deliver. For
this reason, HTTP provides mechanisms for [content
negotiation](#content.negotiation){.xref}.[¶](#section-12-1){.pilcrow}

This specification defines three patterns of content negotiation that
can be made visible within the protocol: \"proactive\" negotiation,
where the server selects the representation based upon the user agent\'s
stated preferences; \"reactive\" negotiation, where the server provides
a list of representations for the user agent to choose from; and
\"request content\" negotiation, where the user agent selects the
representation for a future request based upon the server\'s stated
preferences in past responses.[¶](#section-12-2){.pilcrow}

Other patterns of content negotiation include \"conditional content\",
where the representation consists of multiple parts that are selectively
rendered based on user agent parameters, \"active content\", where the
representation contains a script that makes additional (more specific)
requests based on the user agent characteristics, and \"Transparent
Content Negotiation\" (\[[RFC2295](#RFC2295){.xref}\]), where content
selection is performed by an intermediary. These patterns are not
mutually exclusive, and each has trade-offs in applicability and
practicality.[¶](#section-12-3){.pilcrow}

Note that, in all cases, HTTP is not aware of the resource semantics.
The consistency with which an origin server responds to requests, over
time and over the varying dimensions of content negotiation, and thus
the \"sameness\" of a resource\'s observed representations over time, is
determined entirely by whatever entity or algorithm selects or generates
those responses.[¶](#section-12-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#proactive.negotiation}
::: {#section-12.1 .section}
### [12.1.](#section-12.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Proactive Negotiation](#name-proactive-negotiation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-proactive-negotiation}

When content negotiation preferences are sent by the user agent in a
request to encourage an algorithm located at the server to select the
preferred representation, it is called \"proactive negotiation\"
(a.k.a., \"server-driven negotiation\"). Selection is based on the
available representations for a response (the dimensions over which it
might vary, such as language, content coding, etc.) compared to various
information supplied in the request, including both the explicit
negotiation header fields below and implicit characteristics, such as
the client\'s network address or parts of the
[User-Agent](#field.user-agent){.xref}
field.[¶](#section-12.1-1){.pilcrow}

Proactive negotiation is advantageous when the algorithm for selecting
from among the available representations is difficult to describe to a
user agent, or when the server desires to send its \"best guess\" to the
user agent along with the first response (when that \"best guess\" is
good enough for the user, this avoids the round-trip delay of a
subsequent request). In order to improve the server\'s guess, a user
agent [MAY]{.bcp14} send request header fields that describe its
preferences.[¶](#section-12.1-2){.pilcrow}

Proactive negotiation has serious
disadvantages:[¶](#section-12.1-3){.pilcrow}

-   [It is impossible for the server to accurately determine what might
    be \"best\" for any given user, since that would require complete
    knowledge of both the capabilities of the user agent and the
    intended use for the response (e.g., does the user want to view it
    on screen or print it on
    paper?);[¶](#section-12.1-4.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.1-4.1}
-   [Having the user agent describe its capabilities in every request
    can be both very inefficient (given that only a small percentage of
    responses have multiple representations) and a potential risk to the
    user\'s privacy;[¶](#section-12.1-4.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.1-4.2}
-   [It complicates the implementation of an origin server and the
    algorithms for generating responses to a request;
    and,[¶](#section-12.1-4.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.1-4.3}
-   [It limits the reusability of responses for shared
    caching.[¶](#section-12.1-4.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-12.1-4.4}

A user agent cannot rely on proactive negotiation preferences being
consistently honored, since the origin server might not implement
proactive negotiation for the requested resource or might decide that
sending a response that doesn\'t conform to the user agent\'s
preferences is better than sending a [406 (Not
Acceptable)](#status.406){.xref} response.[¶](#section-12.1-5){.pilcrow}

A [Vary](#field.vary){.xref} header field ([Section
12.5.5](#field.vary){.xref}) is often sent in a response subject to
proactive negotiation to indicate what parts of the request information
were used in the selection algorithm.[¶](#section-12.1-6){.pilcrow}

The request header fields [Accept](#field.accept){.xref},
[Accept-Charset](#field.accept-charset){.xref},
[Accept-Encoding](#field.accept-encoding){.xref}, and
[Accept-Language](#field.accept-language){.xref} are defined below for a
user agent to engage in [proactive
negotiation](#proactive.negotiation){.xref} of the response content. The
preferences sent in these fields apply to any content in the response,
including representations of the target resource, representations of
error or processing status, and potentially even the miscellaneous text
strings that might appear within the
protocol.[¶](#section-12.1-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#reactive.negotiation}
::: {#section-12.2 .section}
### [12.2.](#section-12.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Reactive Negotiation](#name-reactive-negotiation){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-reactive-negotiation}

With \"reactive negotiation\" (a.k.a., \"agent-driven negotiation\"),
selection of content (regardless of the status code) is performed by the
user agent after receiving an initial response. The mechanism for
reactive negotiation might be as simple as a list of references to
alternative representations.[¶](#section-12.2-1){.pilcrow}

If the user agent is not satisfied by the initial response content, it
can perform a GET request on one or more of the alternative resources to
obtain a different representation. Selection of such alternatives might
be performed automatically (by the user agent) or manually (e.g., by the
user selecting from a hypertext menu).[¶](#section-12.2-2){.pilcrow}
